                    if (navigator.connection.saveData != true) {

                        preloadLink.rel = "preload";

                        preloadLink.crossOrigin = "";
                        document.head.insertBefore(preloadLink, document.head.firstChild);
                    }
                }
            }

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41477"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Chromosome-scale assemblies of plant genomes using nanopore long reads and optical maps"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Plants 2018 4:11"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2018-11-02"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2018 The Author(s), under exclusive licence to Springer Nature Limited"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Plant genomes are often characterized by a high level of repetitiveness and polyploid nature. Consequently, creating genome assemblies for plant genomes is challenging. The introduction of short-read technologies 10 years ago substantially increased the number of available plant genomes. Generally, these assemblies are incomplete and fragmented, and only a few are at the chromosome scale. Recently, Pacific Biosciences and Oxford Nanopore sequencing technologies were commercialized that can sequence long DNA fragments (kilobases to megabase) and, using efficient algorithms, provide high-quality assemblies in terms of contiguity and completeness of repetitive regions1&#8211;4. However, even though genome assemblies based on long reads exhibit high contig N50s (&gt;1&#8201;Mb), these methods are still insufficient to decipher genome organization at the chromosome level. Here, we describe a strategy based on long reads (MinION or PromethION sequencers) and optical maps (Saphyr system) that can produce chromosome-level assemblies and demonstrate applicability by generating high-quality genome sequences for two new dicotyledon morphotypes, Brassica rapa Z1 (yellow sarson) and Brassica oleracea HDEM (broccoli), and one new monocotyledon, Musa schizocarpa (banana). All three assemblies show contig N50s of &gt;5&#8201;Mb and contain scaffolds that represent entire chromosomes or chromosome arms. Assembling genomes to chromosome scale remains a challenge. Now, a study reports a strategy based on nanopore long reads and optical maps and uses it to produce high-quality chromosome-scale assemblies for the genomes of yellow sarson, broccoli and banana."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Plant genomes are often characterized by a high level of repetitiveness and polyploid nature. Consequently, creating genome assemblies for plant genomes is challenging. The introduction of short-read technologies 10 years ago substantially increased the number of available plant genomes. Generally, these assemblies are incomplete and fragmented, and only a few are at the chromosome scale. Recently, Pacific Biosciences and Oxford Nanopore sequencing technologies were commercialized that can sequence long DNA fragments (kilobases to megabase) and, using efficient algorithms, provide high-quality assemblies in terms of contiguity and completeness of repetitive regions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Methods 13, 1050–1054 (2016)." href="#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d51054e638">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Jiao, W. B. &amp; Schneeberger, K. The impact of third generation genomic technologies on plant genome assembly. Curr. Opin. Plant. Biol. 36, 64–70 (2017)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d51054e638_1">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Michael, T. P. et al. High contiguity Arabidopsis thaliana genome assembly with a single nanopore flow cell. Nat. Commun. 9, 541 (2018)." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d51054e638_2">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Schmidt, M. H. et al. De novo assembly of a new Solanum pennellii accession using nanopore sequencing. Plant Cell 29, 2336–2348 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d51054e641">4</a></sup>. However, even though genome assemblies based on long reads exhibit high contig N50s (&gt;1 Mb), these methods are still insufficient to decipher genome organization at the chromosome level. Here, we describe a strategy based on long reads (MinION or PromethION sequencers) and optical maps (Saphyr system) that can produce chromosome-level assemblies and demonstrate applicability by generating high-quality genome sequences for two new dicotyledon morphotypes, <i>Brassica rapa</i> Z1 (yellow sarson) and <i>Brassica oleracea</i> HDEM (broccoli), and one new monocotyledon, <i>Musa schizocarpa</i> (banana). All three assemblies show contig N50s of &gt;5 Mb and contain scaffolds that represent entire chromosomes or chromosome arms.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The plant genome epic started with the genomes of two model plants: <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Arabidopsis Genome Initiative Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796–815 (2000)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d51054e678">5</a></sup> for dicotyledons and rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d51054e682">6</a></sup> for monocotyledons in 2000 and 2005, respectively. Their genome sequences, based on the BAC approach and Sanger sequencing, are of high quality and are still today among the best assemblies of plant genomes. With the introduction of Illumina sequencing technology, more than 200 plant genomes have now been sequenced, but most have poor contiguity (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#Fig1">1</a>) and are composed of thousands of scaffolds. Generally, the gene space is relatively complete and correctly assembled, but regions rich in transposable elements are more fragmented or even under-represented. In addition, the dynamics of transposable elements is largely unknown and this knowledge gap is mostly due to the difficulties in assembling repeated elements from genome sequences obtained using short-read technologies. However, with the development of long-read sequencing technologies, such as Oxford Nanopore Technology (ONT) and Pacific Biosciences (PACBIO), the situation is changing radically and these technologies hold great promise for obtaining high-quality assemblies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Methods 13, 1050–1054 (2016)." href="#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d51054e689">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Jiao, W. B. &amp; Schneeberger, K. The impact of third generation genomic technologies on plant genome assembly. Curr. Opin. Plant. Biol. 36, 64–70 (2017)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d51054e689_1">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Michael, T. P. et al. High contiguity Arabidopsis thaliana genome assembly with a single nanopore flow cell. Nat. Commun. 9, 541 (2018)." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d51054e689_2">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Schmidt, M. H. et al. De novo assembly of a new Solanum pennellii accession using nanopore sequencing. Plant Cell 29, 2336–2348 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d51054e692">4</a></sup>. From 105 plant genomes, we observed that, even with long-read strategies, there is still surprisingly high heterogeneity in terms of assembly contiguity. Even today, only a few plant species have a genome assembly with high contiguity. For example, only six species have an assembly with a contig N50 of &gt; 5 Mb—rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793–800 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d51054e696">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Du, H. et al. Sequencing and de novo assembly of a near complete indica rice genome. Nat. Commun. 8, 15324 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d51054e699">7</a></sup>, <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Arabidopsis Genome Initiative Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796–815 (2000)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d51054e706">5</a></sup>, woodland strawberry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Edger, P. P. et al. Single-molecule sequencing and optical mapping yields an improved genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca) with chromosome-scale contiguity. Gigascience 7, 1–7 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d51054e710">8</a></sup>, <i>Schrenkiella</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Dassanayake, M. et al. The genome of the extremophile crucifer Thellungiella parvula. Nat. Genet. 43, 913–918 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d51054e716">9</a></sup>, <i>Brachypodium</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="International Brachypodium Initiative Genome sequencing and analysis of the model grass Brachypodium distachyon. Nature 463, 763–768 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d51054e722">10</a></sup> and <i>Rosa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Raymond, O. et al. The Rosa genome provides new insights into the domestication of modern roses. Nat. Genet. 50, 772–777 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d51054e729">11</a></sup>—and they all have a small genome size (see Methods and Supplementary File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM3">2</a>). Here, using our sequencing strategy, we are able to add three more species to this list: <i>Brassica rapa</i>, <i>Brassica oleracea</i> and <i>Musa schizocarpa</i>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Comparison of contig N50 and genome sizes of 105 existing plant genome assemblies."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Comparison of contig N50 and genome sizes of 105 existing plant genome assemblies.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0289-4/MediaObjects/41477_2018_289_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0289-4/MediaObjects/41477_2018_289_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="268" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>The dots were coloured according to the main sequencing technology used: 454, Illumina, ONT, PACBIO and Sanger.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p><i>Brassica</i> crops include important vegetables for human nutrition and vegetable oil production. Furthermore, they underwent several paleo-polyploidy events, making their current genomes important models for understanding polyploid plants. The recent sequencing of several hundred <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> and <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> genotypes highlighted the fact that similar morphotypes appeared independently in these two species after a whole-genome triplication event and through parallel selection of paralogous genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Cheng, F. et al. Subgenome parallel selection is associated with morphotype diversification and convergent crop domestication in Brassica rapa and Brassica oleracea. Nat. Genet. 48, 1218–1224 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d51054e782">12</a></sup>. This whole-genome triplication contributed to their diversification into heading and tuber-forming morphotypes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Cheng, F. et al. Subgenome parallel selection is associated with morphotype diversification and convergent crop domestication in Brassica rapa and Brassica oleracea. Nat. Genet. 48, 1218–1224 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d51054e787">12</a></sup> (see Methods). It is now accepted that the variability between two morphotypes of the same <i>Brassica</i> species is high, showing the importance of having several reference assemblies for a given species. <i>Musa</i> spp. include dessert and cooking bananas and are essential staple crops in many tropical and subtropical countries and the most popular fruit in industrialized countries. Cultivars are derived from hybridization between <i>Musa</i> species and subspecies, and as such are particularly interesting for studying reticulate evolution. In this context, we decided to sequence two unsequenced morphotypes of <i>Brassica</i> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">1</a>) and the previously unknown genome of <i>M.</i> <i>schizocarpa</i>. The genomes of three relatives of these plants (<i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> Chiifu<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Cai, C. C. et al. Brassica rapa genome 2.0: a reference upgrade through sequence re-assembly and gene re-annotation. Mol. Plant 10, 649–651 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d51054e819">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Wang, X. W. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035–1039 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d51054e822">14</a></sup>, <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> To1000 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Parkin, I. A. et al. Transcriptome and methylome profiling reveals relics of genome dominance in the mesopolyploid Brassica oleracea. Genome Biol. 15, R77 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d51054e833">15</a></sup>) and <i>Musa acuminata</i> Pahang-HD<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="D’Hont, A. et al. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. Nature 488, 213–217 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d51054e840">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Martin, G. et al. Improvement of the banana “Musa acuminata” reference sequence using NGS data and semi-automated bioinformatics methods. BMC Genomics 17, 243 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d51054e843">17</a></sup>) have already been sequenced using short-read strategies, but the resulting assemblies are fragmented (contig N50 of &lt;50 kb) and contain a high proportion of unknown or missing bases (22–30%; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">1A</a>). For example, the initial sequence of the <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> genome lacked near half of the expected genome content (273 of 529 Mb). Even though a recent release<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Cai, C. C. et al. Brassica rapa genome 2.0: a reference upgrade through sequence re-assembly and gene re-annotation. Mol. Plant 10, 649–651 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d51054e857">13</a></sup> improved the situation by adding PACBIO long reads, the assembly is still highly fragmented.</p><p>Here, we de novo sequenced the genomes of <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> (Z1 genotype, estimated size of 529 Mb), <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> (HDEM genotype, estimated size of 630 Mb) and <i>M.</i> <i>schizocarpa</i> (estimated size of 587 Mb) with a strategy combining MinION, which is a portable sequencer commercialized by the Oxford Nanopore company, optical maps produced using the Saphyr system (BioNano Genomics)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Lam, E. T. et al. Genome mapping on nanochannel arrays for structural variation analysis and sequence assembly. Nat. Biotechnol. 30, 771–776 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d51054e883">18</a></sup> and short reads from an Illumina sequencer. First, we generated between 38× and 79× nanopore long reads containing a significant proportion of reads longer than 50 kb, representing between 4.4× and 8.2× coverage (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">2</a>). The resulting long-read assemblies showed high contiguity (less than 1,000 contigs with N50s between 3.8 and 7.3 Mb) that facilitated the use of long-range information provided by the optical maps. The final assemblies had N50s between 5.5 and 9.5 Mb at the contig level and N50s between 15.4 and 36.8 Mb at the scaffold level (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#Tab1">1</a>). Compared with existing assemblies, the contig N50s of our assemblies are between 100-times and 450-times higher, whereas the scaffold N50s are, in general, lower, but the published assemblies were built using genetic maps (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">1B</a>). When adding a genetic map for one of our genomes, <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i>, we were able to deliver an assembly composed of 129 scaffolds, with the nine chromosomes representing 95.3% of the assembly. Importantly, more than 98% of the markers were in accordance in both the assembly and the genetic map (see Methods). Here, we are able to anchor 528.8 Mb, a significant improvement when compared with the 446.8 Mb of the published release. We decided to use comparative genomics based on available related genomes to produce anchored versions of the <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> and <i>M.</i> <i>schizocarpa</i> genomes. However, although we did not compare these final assemblies with existing references as they were not obtained de novo, we did submit these versions to public repositories as they represent valuable resources for the scientific community (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#Fig2">2</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">12</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Statistics of the genome assemblies</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Circular representation of anchored scaffolds of B. oleracea HDEM, B. rapa Z1 and M. schizocarpa genome assemblies."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Circular representation of anchored scaffolds of <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> HDEM, <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> Z1 and <i>M.</i> <i>schizocarpa</i> genome assemblies.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0289-4/MediaObjects/41477_2018_289_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0289-4/MediaObjects/41477_2018_289_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="682" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Rings represent: density of Copia elements (i), density of Gypsy elements (ii), density of transposable elements (iii), gene density (iv), centromeric repeats positions (v) and telomeric repeats positions (vi). Chr, chromosome.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>One-quarter of the chromosomes were composed of a single scaffold and 66% of the chromosomes were assembled into one or two scaffolds, representing either the complete chromosome or a chromosome arm. For example, chromosome 7 of the banana assembly is spanned by a single scaffold that harbours telomeric repeats at both extremities and a high density of centromeric repeats in a 4-Mb region (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">2</a>), representing a real improvement compared with the available reference. As observed on this particular chromosome, long-read assembly generated a mix of large and small contigs, proving the importance of combining long reads with long-range information to decipher the chromosome architecture.</p><p>We then performed gene prediction on our three genome assemblies using existing annotations of closely related species (see Methods). We annotated 46,721, 61,279 and 32,809 genes for <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i>, <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> and <i>M.</i> <i>schizocarpa</i>, respectively (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#Tab1">1</a>), consistent with the available gene sets and the evolutionary history of these genomes. Transposable elements and, more generally, transposable element-rich regions are under-represented in short-read assemblies, and as expected, in the long-read assemblies, we detected a higher proportion of bases accounting for long interspersed nuclear element (LINE), long terminal repeat retrotransposon and DNA transposon families and the average sizes of the detected transposable elements were higher (see Methods and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">10</a>). For example, we predicted 14.95%, 37.95% and 59.95% more complete copies of Copia elements in our assemblies (<i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i>, <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> and <i>M.</i> <i>schizocarpa</i>, respectively) than the reference genomes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">15</a>). The gene content was mostly the same between the short-read and long-read assemblies, but the long-read assemblies improved the completeness of the transposable element catalogue as well as the genomic context of these transposable element-rich regions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#Fig3">3</a>). Generally, genes inserted in transposable element-rich regions are hard to anchor on the chromosomes, and again, our long-read assemblies made it possible to anchor a higher proportion of genes, more than 98% for the three assemblies (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">12</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Base annotation of the three ONT genomes and the corresponding current references (B. rapa, B. oleracea and Musa species)."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Base annotation of the three ONT genomes and the corresponding current references (<i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i>, <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> and <i>Musa</i> species).</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0289-4/MediaObjects/41477_2018_289_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0289-4/MediaObjects/41477_2018_289_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="356" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Genomic regions were classified into several categories: repeats, introns, exons, gaps (Ns) and unannotated regions. Repeated elements were divided into five classes: DNA transposons, LINEs, Copia, Gypsy and other known transposable elements (TEs).</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Read length is a key factor in improving the assembly of transposable element-rich regions and, as a consequence, the assembly contiguity. Recent plant assemblies based on PACBIO sequencing show lower N50s at the contig level (except for the <i>Rosa chinensis</i> assembly) owing to the difficulty of sequencing long DNA fragments. We compared the recent <i>Vigna angularis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Sakai, H. et al. The power of single molecule real-time sequencing technology in the de novo assembly of a eukaryotic genome. Sci. Rep. 5, 16780 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d51054e1931">19</a></sup>, <i>Vitis vinifera</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Chin, C. S. et al. Phased diploid genome assembly with single-molecule real-time sequencing. Nat. Methods 13, 1050–1054 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d51054e1937">1</a></sup>, <i>Citrus maxima</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Wang, X. et al. Genomic analyses of primitive, wild and cultivated citrus provide insights into asexual reproduction. Nat. Genet. 49, 765–772 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d51054e1943">20</a></sup>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Berlin, K. et al. Assembling large genomes with single-molecule sequencing and locality-sensitive hashing. Nat. Biotechnol. 33, 623–630 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d51054e1950">21</a></sup>, <i>Fragaria vesca</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Edger, P. P. et al. Single-molecule sequencing and optical mapping yields an improved genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca) with chromosome-scale contiguity. Gigascience 7, 1–7 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d51054e1956">8</a></sup> and <i>R.</i> <i>chinensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Raymond, O. et al. The Rosa genome provides new insights into the domestication of modern roses. Nat. Genet. 50, 772–777 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d51054e1965">11</a></sup> PACBIO data with our three ONT data sets and observed a higher proportion of long reads (&gt;50 kb) in the ONT data (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">3</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">17</a>). Moreover, the PACBIO coverage was higher (between 125× and 283×), suggesting the need for higher coverage to obtain a sufficient number of long reads to perform high-contiguity genome assemblies. The nine genomes have estimated genome sizes of 130–630 Mb, and the best assemblies (in terms of contiguity) were the ones produced with the longest reads. Interestingly, the second-most contiguous (contig N50 of 9.5 Mb) assembly was obtained with a long-read data set that had the smallest coverage (~36×) and the longest reads (reads N50 of 31 kb), showing the higher effect of read length over coverage and confirming the possibility of producing high-quality assemblies with 30× long reads coverage<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Schmidt, M. H. et al. De novo assembly of a new Solanum pennellii accession using nanopore sequencing. Plant Cell 29, 2336–2348 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d51054e1976">4</a></sup>. This low-coverage requirement will surely be reduced thanks to the ongoing improvement of the nanopore technology and of protocols for DNA extraction, which still represents a real challenge for numerous plant species.</p><p>MinION is a low-cost sequencer, but the current throughput, although sufficient to sequence eukaryotic genomes, is still a limitation to reducing sequencing costs. Our chromosome-scale assemblies cost on average US$14,071 (see Methods) for an average genome size of 582 Mb. However, ONT is currently launching a high-throughput platform named PromethION that promises to lower the cost of sequencing eukaryotic genomes. Here, we sequenced the <i>M.</i> <i>schizocarpa</i> genome using a single flow cell that produced 17.6 Gb of data with a comparable read N50 size (26 kb versus 24 kb with MinION; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">2</a>). We assembled this PromethION data set using the same protocol (based on long reads, short reads and two optical maps) and obtained an assembly of the same quality (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#Tab1">1</a>). This first attempt using the PromethION device reduced the sequencing cost from US$16,300 to US$6,500 for the banana genome.</p><p>To highlight the importance of using these new <i>Brassica</i> assemblies as reference genomes, we aligned the resequencing data of 199 <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> and 119 <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> accessions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Cheng, F. et al. Subgenome parallel selection is associated with morphotype diversification and convergent crop domestication in Brassica rapa and Brassica oleracea. Nat. Genet. 48, 1218–1224 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2014">12</a></sup> on both the existing references and our genomes. These 318 <i>Brassica</i> accessions represent various morphotypes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">1</a>), with some closer to the reference genomes (Chinese cabbage for <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> Chiifu and Chinese kale for <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> To1000) and others closer to our Z1 and HDEM accessions (sarsons for <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> and broccoli for <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i>). However, we surprisingly observed that we were able to map a higher proportion of reads on our assemblies (0.61% more for <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> and 2.77% more for <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> on average) for all of the accessions regardless of the morphotype, except for the Chinese cabbage accessions of <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">13</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">18</a>). Furthermore, as expected, the proportion of uniquely mapped reads was lower on our assemblies, suggesting that repeats were collapsed in the reference genomes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">19</a>). We expertized the reads that could not be mapped to the reference genomes and detected 1.14 Mb and 1.54 Mb (in HDEM and Z1, respectively) of genic regions that were specific to our accessions or missing from the reference chromosomes (see Methods). These results promote our new genome assemblies as prime references for resequencing analysis.</p><p>Even if the <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> and <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> pair of genomes are highly conserved, we detected several differences at the gene level. A comparison of orthologous proteins from Z1–Chiifu and HDEM–To1000 pairs revealed a higher conservation between <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> morphotypes (median identity per cent of 99.2% and 98.9%, respectively; Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">16</a>). Similarly, when looking at the gene-order conservation, we identified more translocation events of gene blocks in the Z1–Chiifu pair (23 against 1; see Methods and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">7</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">9</a>).</p><p>We searched for the Flowering Locus C (<i>FLC</i>) genes, which are known to be responsible for vernalization and flowering time. Copy number variations of this gene family seem to affect the flowering time. Here, we found, as expected in a broccoli morphotype, the <i>FLC1</i>, a partial <i>FLC2</i> (the disrupted <i>FLC2</i> allele in cauliflower was associated with early flowering) and <i>FLC3</i> genes and, interestingly, we annotated an <i>FLC5</i> gene (reported as specific to the cauliflower morphotype<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Golicz, A. A. et al. The pangenome of an agronomically important crop plant Brassica oleracea. Nat. Commun. 7, 13390 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2131">22</a></sup>) and three tandemly duplicated copies of <i>FLC1</i>, as suggested in a previous study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Schranz, M. E. et al. Characterization and effects of the replicated flowering time gene FLC in Brassica rapa. Genetics 162, 1457–1468 (2002)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2138">23</a></sup>. In comparison, we found four <i>FLC</i> genes in <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> Z1, as expected (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">17</a>). Furthermore, we investigated the <i>S</i>-locus, a 30–150-kb region that is strongly enriched in transposable elements<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Goubet, P. M. et al. Contrasted patterns of molecular evolution in dominant and recessive self-incompatibility haplotypes in Arabidopsis. PLoS Genet. 8, e1002495 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2158">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Shiba, H. et al. Genomic organization of the S-locus region of Brassica. Biosci. Biotechnol. Biochem. 67, 622–626 (2003)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2161">25</a></sup>, which causes major difficulties when attempting to assemble it in its entirety using short reads<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Bachmann, J. A., Tedder, A., Laenen, B., Steige, K. A. &amp; Slotte, T. Targeted long-read sequencing of a locus under long-term balancing selection in Capsella. G3 (Bethesda) 8, 1327–1333 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2165">26</a></sup>. For example, the <i>S</i>-locus in the <i>Raphanus sativus</i> genome assembly is spread over several contigs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Kim, D., Jung, J., Choi, Y. O. &amp; Kim, S. Development of a system for S locus haplotyping based on the polymorphic SLL2 gene tightly linked to the locus determining self-incompatibility in radish (Raphanus sativus L.). Euphytica 209, 525–535 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2176">27</a></sup>, whereas that of <i>Brassica nigra</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Yang, J. H. et al. The genome sequence of allopolyploid Brassica juncea and analysis of differential homoeolog gene expression influencing selection. Nat. Genet. 48, 1225–1232 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2182">28</a></sup> is spread over 2.2 Mb, suggesting problems with the assembly. We identified a full <i>S</i>-locus in <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> Z1 spanning a 48-kb region syntenic to the <i>S</i>-locus region (53 kb, chromosome A07) in the Chiifu assembly. For <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> HDEM, the <i>S</i>-locus spanned a 102-kb region similar to the <i>S</i>-locus region (71 kb, chromosome C06) of To1000 (see Methods and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">18</a>).</p><p>Whole-genome comparison of <i>M.</i> <i>schizocarpa</i> with <i>M.</i> <i>acuminata</i> revealed high variability in the centromeric regions (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">4</a>). Both versions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="D’Hont, A. et al. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. Nature 488, 213–217 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2235">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Martin, G. et al. Improvement of the banana “Musa acuminata” reference sequence using NGS data and semi-automated bioinformatics methods. BMC Genomics 17, 243 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2238">17</a></sup> of the <i>M.</i> <i>acuminata</i> genome were more fragmented and were anchored using a genetic map. Centromeric regions have a low recombination rate; thus, sequences originating from these regions are always difficult to order and orient correctly, although they represent an essential component of the genomic landscape. This observation highlights the importance of having large contigs to locate centromeres and the richness of the information provided by an optical map compared with a conventional genetic map. Furthermore, we examined disease resistance-like genes (<i>R</i>-genes), which are organized in clusters and are generally difficult to assemble correctly. We compared the proportion of undetermined nucleotides for three orthologous <i>R</i>-gene clusters on both the <i>M.</i> <i>acuminata</i> and the <i>M.</i> <i>schizocarpa</i> genomes (see Methods). We found a clear difference between the two genome assemblies (6.5% and 0% of undetermined bases for <i>M.</i> <i>acuminata</i> and <i>M.</i> <i>schizocarpa</i>, respectively), showing again the importance of long reads for resolving complex regions. A comparison of orthologous proteins showed a high conservation level, both at the base level (median identity per cent of 98.0% between orthologues) and the synteny level (five translocation events, due to assembly errors in the first <i>M.</i> <i>acuminata</i> assembly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Martin, G. et al. Improvement of the banana “Musa acuminata” reference sequence using NGS data and semi-automated bioinformatics methods. BMC Genomics 17, 243 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2286">17</a></sup>) (see Methods and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">14</a>).</p><p>We demonstrated that combining three technologies (ONT, BioNano Genomics and Illumina) can lead to high-quality and relatively low-cost genome assemblies when using the PromethION device (around US$6,000 for 500–600 Mb genomes). We present high-quality genomes for three plant genomes (two Brassicaceae and one banana species) and, when compared with existing reference genomes, our assemblies provide a real improvement, especially in regions enriched in transposable elements. We annotated the three assemblies and observed similar gene content in the gene-rich regions, but a more complete catalogue of transposable elements was produced and the <i>S</i>-loci of the two <i>Brassica</i> could be entirely annotated thanks to the high quality of the assemblies in these regions. Further improvements are still needed to enable extraction of high-molecular-weight DNA for all plants, and systematic errors in the nanopore long reads mean that Illumina sequences are still required to polish assemblies. Today, optical maps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Edger, P. P. et al. Single-molecule sequencing and optical mapping yields an improved genome of woodland strawberry (Fragaria vesca) with chromosome-scale contiguity. Gigascience 7, 1–7 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2305">8</a></sup> or chromosome conformation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Jarvis, D. E. et al. The genome of Chenopodium quinoa. Nature 542, 307–312 (2017)." href="#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2309">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Jiao, W. B. et al. Improving and correcting the contiguity of long-read genome assemblies of three plant species using optical mapping and chromosome conformation capture data. Genome Res. 27, 778–786 (2017)." href="#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2309_1">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Reyes-Chin-Wo, S. et al. Genome assembly with in vitro proximity ligation data and whole-genome triplication in lettuce. Nat. Commun. 8, 14953 (2017)." href="#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2309_2">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Teh, B. T. et al. The draft genome of tropical fruit durian (Durio zibethinus). Nat. Genet. 49, 1633–1641 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2312">32</a></sup> capture is still mandatory to propose chromosome-scale assemblies for large plant genomes. Even though extraction of high-molecular-weight DNA could remain a challenge, one can imagine that read lengths will increase, enabling the assembly of complete chromosomes with long-read sequencing in coming years.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">
                           <i>Brassica</i> morphotypes</h3><p>Among the large diversity described for both <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> and <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i>, domestication gave rise around 500 years ago to highly contrasted morphotypes for tubers, heads or seeds. Among the six distinct genetic groups identified for <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Cheng, F. et al. Subgenome parallel selection is associated with morphotype diversification and convergent crop domestication in Brassica rapa and Brassica oleracea. Nat. Genet. 48, 1218–1224 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2350">12</a></sup>, the genotype ‘Chiifu’ used for the reference genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Wang, X. W. et al. The genome of the mesopolyploid crop species Brassica rapa. Nat. Genet. 43, 1035–1039 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2354">14</a></sup> is a heading type and belongs to the clade that is the most divergent to the last <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> common ancestor. On the contrary, the genotype sequenced in this study (Z1) is a sarson type (oilseed type) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">1</a>) and is much closer to the <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> root. Similarly, the genotype used in this study (HDEM: broccoli) belongs to a different morphotype than the <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> ‘To1000’ reference genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Parkin, I. A. et al. Transcriptome and methylome profiling reveals relics of genome dominance in the mesopolyploid Brassica oleracea. Genome Biol. 15, R77 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2380">15</a></sup> (Chinese kale) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Plants</h3><p><i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> ssp. <i>trilocularis</i> Z1 and <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> ssp. <i>botrytis italica</i> HDEM seeds were sown in Fertiss blocks (Fertil). Plantlets were grown under a 16-h light/8-h night photoperiod in a greenhouse at 20 °C for 10–12 days. Prior to harvest, the plants were either dark treated for 5 days or not treated after the 10–12-day culture.</p><p><i>M.</i> <i>schizocarpa</i> (ITC926) in vitro plants were obtained from the International Musa Germplasm Collection hold at the International Transit Centre (ITC, Leuven, Belgium). The plants were grown under natural light in a greenhouse at about 25 °C (minimum: 15 °C, maximum: 50 °C), in 1 l pots until they reached a height of 50 cm and had five fully expanded leaves. Harvesting was performed in a period comprised between 8 months and 1 year after the initiation of culture.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">DNA extraction</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec6">
                              <i>M.</i> <i>schizocarpa</i> DNA extraction</h4><p>High-molecular-weight plant DNA extraction was performed using a modified mixed alkyl trimethyl ammonium bromide (MATAB) procedure<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Gawel, N. J. &amp; Jarret, R. L. A modified CTAB DNA extraction procedure for Musa and Ipomoea. Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 9, 262–266 (1991)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2439">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Risterucci, A. M. et al. A high-density linkage map of Theobroma cacao L. Theor. Appl. Genet. 101, 948–955 (2000)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2442">34</a></sup>. A total of 2 g freshly harvested leaves was ground in liquid nitrogen with a mortar and pestle and immediately transferred to 12 ml of 74 °C prewarmed extraction buffer containing 100 mM Tris-HCl, pH 8, 20 mM EDTA, 1.4 M NaCl, 2% w/v MATAB, 1% w/v PEG6000 (polyethylene glycol), 0.5% w/v sodium sulfite and 20 mg l<sup>−1</sup> RNAse A. Crude extracts were maintained for 20 min at 74 °C, extracted with an equal volume of chloroform-isoamylalcohol (24:1) and transferred to clean tubes. DNA was recovered by centrifugation after adding 10 ml isopropanol. DNA precipitates were briefly dried, washed with 2 ml of 70% ethanol and resuspended in 1 ml sterile water. Extract quality was evaluated using pulse field gel electrophoresis for size estimation and spectrophotometry (A260/A280 and A260/A230 ratios) for purity estimation. DNA samples with a fragment size above 50 kb, a A260/A280 ratio close to 2 and a A260/A230 ratio above 1.5 were kept.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec7">
                              <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> and <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> DNA extraction</h4><p>For some of the nanopore sequencing runs, <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> Z1 and <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> HDEM DNA extracts were prepared according to the protocol used for optical maps (see Optical maps).</p><p>For the other sequencing runs, 1 cm<sup>2</sup> first young leaves were harvested and the mid-ribs were removed. The samples were placed on aluminium foil on ice. Then, 2.5 g of each genotype was ground in liquid nitrogen with a mortar and pestle for 1 min. The ground materials were homogenized with 10 ml pre-heated CF lysis buffer (MACHEREY-NAGEL) supplemented with 4 mg proteinase K in 50-ml tubes containing phase-lock gel and incubated for 45 min at 56 °C. Next, 10 ml saturated phenol (25:24:1) was added to the samples and the tubes were placed on a rotator at 40 r.p.m. for 10 min to get a fine emulsion. The samples were centrifuged for 24 min at 4,500<i>g</i> (Acc3/Dec3) and the aqueous phases were poured into new 50-ml tubes containing phase-lock gel. Subsequently, 10 ml chloroform-octanol (24:1) was added to each sample and the tubes were placed on a rotator at 40 r.p.m. for 10 min to get a fine emulsion. The samples were centrifuged for 24 min at 4,500<i>g</i> (Acc3/Dec3) and the aqueous phases were poured into new 50-ml tubes. The DNA was precipitated by adding 4 ml of 5 M NaCl and 30 ml of cold 100% isopropanol. After 3 h at 4 °C, the DNA was removed in one piece with a hook produced by melting a glass capillary in a blue flame. The DNA pellets were submerged in 50-ml tubes containing 70% ethanol and transferred to new tubes to evaporate the remaining isopropanol at 37 °C (in an oven). The dried DNA was resuspended with 3 ml transposable element 10/1 buffer. The extract quality was evaluated using field inverted gel electrophoresis with the Pippin pulse system (Sage Sciences). DNA samples with a fragment size above 50 kb were kept and run on BluePippin (Sage Sciences).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Illumina sequencing</h3><p>DNA (1.5 μg) was sonicated to a 100–1,500-bp size range using a Covaris E220 sonicator (Covaris). The fragments were end repaired and 3′-adenylated, and Illumina adapters were added using the Kapa Hyper Prep Kit (Kapa Biosystems). The ligation products were purified with AMPure XP beads (Beckmann Coulter Genomics). The libraries were quantified by quantitative PCR using the KAPA Library Quantification Kit for Illumina Libraries (Kapa Biosystems), and the library profiles were assessed using a DNA High Sensitivity LabChip kit on an Agilent Bioanalyzer (Agilent Technologies). The libraries were sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq2500 instrument (Illumina) using 250 base length read chemistry in paired-end mode.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Nanopore sequencing</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec10">MinION</h4><p>Most of the libraries were prepared according to the following protocol, using the Oxford Nanopore SQK-LSK108 kit. Genomic DNA or DNA previously fragmented to 50 kb with a Megaruptor (Diagenode S.A.) was first size selected using BluePippin (Sage Sciences). The selected DNA fragments were end repaired and 3′-adenylated with the NEBNext Ultra II End Repair/dA-Tailing Module (New England Biolabs). The DNA was then purified with AMPure XP beads (Beckmann Coulter) and ligated with sequencing adapters provided by ONT using the Blunt/TA Ligase Master Mix (NEB). After purification with AMPure XP beads, the library was mixed with running buffer with fuel mix (ONT) and library loading beads (ONT) and loaded on MinION R9.4 or R9.5 SpotON Flow Cells.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec11">PromethION</h4><p>Libraries were prepared following the Oxford Nanopore ‘1D Genomic DNA by ligation (Kit 9 chemistry)—PromethION’ protocol. Genomic DNA was first repaired and end prepped with NEBNext FFPE Repair Mix (New England Biolabs) and the NEBNext Ultra II End Repair/dA-Tailing Module (NEB). The DNA was then purified with AMPure XP beads (Beckmann Coulter) and ligated with sequencing adapters provided by ONT using concentrated T4 DNA ligase 2 M U ml<sup>−1</sup> (NEB). After purification with AMPure XP beads (Beckman Coulter) using dilution buffer (ONT) and wash buffer (ONT), the library was mixed with sequencing buffer (ONT) and library loading beads (ONT) and loaded on the PromethION flow cells.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Optical maps</h3><p>For <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> Z1 and <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> HDEM, 1 cm<sup>2</sup> first young leaves were harvested and the mid-ribs were removed. The samples were placed on aluminium foil on ice. Then, 5 g of each genotype was ground in liquid nitrogen with a mortar and pestle for 2 min. The ground materials were homogenized in 50 ml NIBTM (10 mM Tris-HCl, pH 8, 10 mM EDTA, pH 8, 80 mM KCl, 0.5 M sucrose, 1 mM spermine tetrahydrochloride, 1 mM spermidine trihydrochloride and 2% (w/v) PVP40), the pH was adjusted to 9.4 and the solution was filtered through a 0.22-µm filter (NIB) and supplemented with 0.5% Triton X-100 (NIBT) and 7.5% 2-mercaptoethanol (NIBTM). The nuclei suspensions were filtered through cheese cloth and Mira cloth and centrifuged at 1,500<i>g</i> for 20 min at 4 °C. The pellets were suspended in 1 ml NIBTM and adjusted to 20 ml with NIBTM. The nuclei suspensions were filtered again through cheese cloth and Mira cloth and centrifuged at 57<i>g</i> for 2 min at 4 °C. The supernatants were kept and centrifuged at 1,500<i>g</i> for 20 min at 4 °C. The pellets were suspended in 1 ml NIBT and adjusted to 20 ml with NIBT. To wash the pellets, the last steps were repeated three times with 50 ml NIBT and a final time with 50 ml NIB. The pellets were suspended in residual NIB (approximately 200 µl), transferred to a 1.5-ml tube and centrifuged at 1,500<i>g</i> for 2 min at 4 °C. The nuclei were suspended in cell suspension buffer from CHEF Genomic DNA Plug Kits (Bio-Rad), and melted 2% agarose from the same kit was added to reach a 0.75% agarose plug concentration. Plug lysis and DNA retrieval were performed as recommended by Bionano Genomics.</p><p>For <i>M.</i> <i>schizocarpa</i>, a young cigar leaf was harvested and the mid-rib was removed. Only the yellow part was used. Then, 2 g of the leaf segment was cut to 2 × 2 cm and fixed in 2% formaldehyde according to the Bionano Genomics protocol, except the fixation step was performed in a vacuum bell. After the 100-µm and 40-µm filter steps, the nuclei suspension was centrifuged at 60<i>g</i> for 2 min at 4 °C. The supernatant was filtered again through a 40-µm filter. The last centrifugation was repeated and the supernatant was filtered again through a 40-µm filter. The nuclei suspension was centrifuged at 400<i>g</i> for 15 min at 4 °C. The pellet was suspended in residual buffer and adjusted to 35 ml. The nuclei suspension was centrifuged once more at 400<i>g</i> for 15 min at 4 °C. The pellet was suspended in residual buffer and adjusted to 35 ml. The nuclei suspension was centrifuged at 200<i>g</i> and the supernatant was kept and centrifuged at 400<i>g</i> for 10 min at 4 °C. The nuclei were suspended in homogenisation buffer plus (Bionano Genomics), and melted 2% agarose from CHEF Genomic DNA Plug Kits (Bio-Rad) was added to reach a 0.82% agarose plug concentration. Plug lysis was performed with Bionano lysis buffer adjusted to pH 9 and supplemented with 0.4% 2-mercaptoethanol. DNA retrieval was performed as recommended by Bionano Genomics.</p><p>The Nicks, Labels, Repairs and Stains labelling (BspQI) protocols were performed for <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> Z1, <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> HDEM and <i>M.</i> <i>schizocarpa</i> according to Bionano Genomics with 600, 300 and 191.2 ng DNA, respectively. For the direct label and stain (DLS) labelling (DLE-1), <i>M.</i> <i>schizocarpa</i> DNA was concentrated by evaporation at room temperature. All DLS labelling was performed with 750 ng DNA. Chip loading was performed as recommended by Bionano Genomics.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Quality control of raw reads</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec14">Illumina data</h4><p>After Illumina sequencing, an in-house quality control process was applied to the reads that passed the Illumina quality filters. The first step discarded low-quality nucleotides (Q &lt; 20) from both ends of the reads. Next, Illumina sequencing adapters and primer sequences were removed from the reads. Then, reads shorter than 30 nucleotides after trimming were discarded. These trimming and removal steps were achieved using in-house-designed software based on the FastX package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Engelen, S. &amp; Aury J. M. Fastxtend tool (Genoscope/CEA, 2015); &#xA;                    http://www.genoscope.cns.fr/fastxtend/&#xA;                    &#xA;                  &#xA;                        " href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2625">35</a></sup>. The last step identified and discarded read pairs that mapped to the phage phiX genome, using SOAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Li, R., Li, Y., Kristiansen, K. &amp; Wang, J. SOAP: short oligonucleotide alignment program. Bioinformatics 24, 713–714 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2629">36</a></sup> and the phiX reference sequence (GenBank: NC_001422.1). This processing resulted in high-quality data and improvement of subsequent analyses.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec15">Nanopore data</h4><p>The nanopore long reads were not cleaned; we used the raw reads for each genome assembly. Taxonomic assignation was performed using Centrifuge<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Kim, D., Song, L., Breitwieser, F. P. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Centrifuge: rapid and sensitive classification of metagenomic sequences. Genome Res. 26, 1721–1729 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2641">37</a></sup> for each data set to detect potential contamination.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Long-read genome assemblies</h3><p>We used the Ra<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Vaser, R. et al. Ra assembler. v. git commit 65bedfe (Faculty of Electrical Engineering and Computing, University of Zagreb, 2017); &#xA;                    https://github.com/rvaser/ra&#xA;                    &#xA;                  &#xA;                        " href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2654">38</a></sup>, SMARTdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Ruan, J. et al. SMARTdenovo assembler. v. git commit 3d9c22e (Agricultral Genomics Insititute, China, 2015) ; &#xA;                    https://github.com/ruanjue/smartdenovo&#xA;                    &#xA;                  &#xA;                        " href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2658">39</a></sup> and wtdbg assemblers with all nanopore raw (or corrected) reads or subsets of raw (or corrected) reads composed of either the longest reads or those selected by the Filtlong<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Wick, R. et al. Fitlong tool. v. git commit 8d81024 (University of Melbourne, 2017); &#xA;                    https://github.com/rrwick/Filtlong&#xA;                    &#xA;                  &#xA;                        " href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2662">40</a></sup> software (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">3</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">5</a>), as it has been proven that downsampling the read coverage can be beneficial for the assembly phase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Schmidt, M. H. et al. De novo assembly of a new Solanum pennellii accession using nanopore sequencing. Plant Cell 29, 2336–2348 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2673">4</a></sup>. We also tried to use Canu<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722–736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2677">41</a></sup> but could not get to the final assembly stage owing to the high computational requirements. Moreover, we could not select any subsets of reads for <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i>, as the sequencing depth was too low to subsample the sequencing data. Ra, wtdbg and Filtlong were used with default parameters. We used the following options as inputs to SMARTdenovo: ‘-c 1’ to generate a consensus sequence, ‘-J 5000’ to remove sequences smaller than 5 kb and ‘-k 17’ to use 17-mers, as this is advised by the developers for large genomes.</p><p>Then, we selected the ‘best’ assembly for each organism, based on contiguity metrics, such as N50 or cumulative size. For all organisms, the Ra assembler produced the most contiguous assembly. The best <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> and <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> assemblies were obtained using all of the reads and had contig N50s of 7.3 Mb and 3.8 Mb, respectively. By contrast, the best <i>M.</i> <i>schizocarpa</i> assembly (N50 of 4.0 Mb) was obtained using a 30× subset of reads generated by Filtlong.</p><p>A high-quality consensus was needed for both aligning the optical map onto the contigs and annotating genes. As nanopore reads contain systematic errors in homopolymeric regions, we polished the consensus of the selected assembly three times with the nanopore reads as input to the Racon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Vaser, R., Sovic, I., Nagarajan, N. &amp; Sikic, M. Fast and accurate de novo genome assembly from long uncorrected reads. Genome Res. 27, 737–746 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2712">42</a></sup> software and then three additional times using Illumina reads as input to the Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2716">43</a></sup> tool (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">6</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">8</a>). Both tools were used with default parameters. The polishing process significantly improved the number of complete BUSCOs detected in all organisms. The percentage of complete BUSCOs went from 74.2% to 97.3% for <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i>, from 79.7% to 97.8% for <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> and from 53.8% to 93.4% for <i>M.</i> <i>schizocarpa</i>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Long-range genome assemblies</h3><p>Two enzymes (BspQI and DLE-1) were used to generate optical maps and both maps were produced using a single chip for each genome. The DLE-1 map was generated using the new DLS technology, which significantly improved the contiguity of the optical maps (N50s are 6–15-times higher using DLS; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">10</a>). Genome map assemblies for the three species were generated using Bionano Solve Pipeline version 3.1.1 and Bionano Access version 1.0a. A rough assembly was first performed with the following parameters: -i 0 -V 0 -A -z -u -m (pipelineCL.py). This first result was used as a reference for a second assembly, launched with the following parameters (as recommended by the supplier): -y -r (rough assembly cmap) -V 0 -m. We filtered out molecules smaller than 180 kb and molecules with less than nine labelling sites (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">10</a>). The nanopore contigs were then organized using the two Bionano maps (DLE and BspQI), with the scaffolding procedure provided by BioNano Genomics, and negative gap sizes were checked with an internal procedure that fused overlapping contigs and greatly improved the contig size (see the ‘Resolution of negative gaps’ section).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Construction of a high-density <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> genetic map and validation and anchoring of our <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> assembly</h3><p>To construct a <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> genetic map, an F2 population (95 progenies) was obtained from a cross between Richelain (<i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> ssp. <i>capitata</i>) and HDEM (<i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> var. <i>botrytis italica</i>). This population was genotyped using the Illumina 60 K array and a genetic map was constructed using the CarthaGène software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="de Givry, S., Bouchez, M., Chabrier, P., Milan, D. &amp; Schiex, T. CARHTA GENE: multipopulation integrated genetic and radiation hybrid mapping. Bioinformatics 21, 1703–1704 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2808">44</a></sup>. A total of 6,528 markers were genetically mapped, totalling 817.3 cm. The sequence contexts of all single-nucleotide polymorphism markers that were genetically mapped were blasted against our <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> HDEM assembly to validate the quality of our assembly and to help with the ordering and orientation of scaffolds. Of these 6,528 markers, 5,449 were physically anchored on the HDEM assembly and, more specifically, onto the 20 largest scaffolds (out of 140), representing 96.96% of the whole assembly. The genetic and physical positions were discordant for only 95 markers (1.74%) due to an inaccurate position on the genetic map (of a few centimorgans in almost all cases). In most cases, only two scaffolds per pseudomolecule were obtained, with one end of each scaffold corresponding to a centromere region (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">12</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Resolution of negative gaps</h3><p>We inspected several regions of the optical map where two nanopore contigs were joined and found in several cases that the nanopore contigs overlapped (based on the optical map), and this overlap was not managed by the hybrid scaffolding procedure (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">11</a>). In these cases, the workflow decided not to fuse the two contigs and added a 499-bp gap. We checked all 499-bp gaps and aligned both 30-kb flanking regions with BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Kent, W. J. BLAT—the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656–664 (2002)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2834">45</a></sup>. The two flanking contigs were joined if one alignment (score of &gt;3,000) was detected. This procedure resolved several negative gaps and improved the contig N50 (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">11</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Transposable element annotation</h3><p>Transposable elements and, more generally, transposable element-rich regions are under-represented in short-read assemblies. To investigate this aspect, we performed transposable element detection for our three genomes and the three available reference assemblies.
