Transposable elements were annotated using RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="RepeatMasker Open-4. 0 (Institute for Systems Biology, 2013); &#xA;                    http://www.repeatmasker.org&#xA;                    &#xA;                  &#xA;                        " href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2849">46</a></sup> (with default parameters, taxon viridiplantae for <i>M.</i> <i>schizocarpa</i> and eudicotyledons for <i>Brassica</i>) and transposable element libraries. The transposable element database generated in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Plant genetics. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950–953 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2862">47</a></sup> was used to annotate the <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> and <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> genomes. The transposable element database for the <i>M.</i> <i>schizocarpa</i> annotation came from an <i>M.</i> <i>acuminata</i> study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Martin, G. et al. Improvement of the banana “Musa acuminata” reference sequence using NGS data and semi-automated bioinformatics methods. BMC Genomics 17, 243 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d51054e2892">17</a></sup>. We masked 34.43%, 37.82% and 51.09% of the genomes of <i>B.</i> <i>rapa, B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> and <i>M.</i> <i>schizocarpa</i>, respectively (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">15</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">16</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Gene prediction</h3><p>Gene prediction was done using proteomes from homologous species. For <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i>, we used the following three proteomes: <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> (UP000011750), <i>Brassica</i> <i>napus</i> (UP000028999) and <i>A.</i> <i>thaliana</i> (UP000006548). For <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i>, we used the proteomes of <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> (UP000032141), <i>B.</i> <i>napus</i> and <i>A.</i> <i>thaliana</i>. For <i>M.</i> <i>schizocarpa</i>, we used the proteomes of <i>M.</i> <i>acuminate</i> (UP000012960), <i>Oryza</i> <i>sativa</i> (UP000059680) and <i>Phoenix dactylifera</i> (UP000228380).</p><p>Low complexity in protein sequences was masked with the SEG algorithm. Low complexity in genomic sequences was masked using the DustMasker algorithm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Morgulis, A., Gertz, E. M., Schaffer, A. A. &amp; Agarwala, R. A fast and symmetric DUST implementation to mask low-complexity DNA sequences. J. Comput. Biol. 13, 1028–1040 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d51054e3001">48</a></sup>. Tandem repeats were masked using Tandem Repeat Finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573–580 (1999)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d51054e3005">49</a></sup>. The transposable elements detected by RepeatMasker were also masked for the gene prediction step, as described in the ‘Transposable element annotation’ section.</p><p>The proteomes were aligned to the genomes in two steps. First, BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Kent, W. J. BLAT—the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656–664 (2002)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d51054e3012">45</a></sup> (default parameters) was used to quickly localize corresponding putative genes of the proteins on the genome. The best match and matches with a score of ≥90% (70% for <i>A.</i> <i>thaliana</i> proteins) of the best-match score were retained. Second, the alignments were refined using Genewise<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and Genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988–995 (2004)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d51054e3022">50</a></sup> (default parameters), which is more precise for intron–exon boundary detection. Alignments were kept if more than 80% of the length of the protein was aligned to the genome.</p><p>We integrated the protein homologies using a combiner called Gmove<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Dubarry, M. et al. Gmove a tool for eukaryotic gene predictions using various evidences (poster). F1000Res. 5, 681 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d51054e3029">51</a></sup> to predict gene structures. This tool can find coding sequences based on the protein mapping structures. It is easy to use with no need for a pre-calibration step. Briefly, putative exons and introns, extracted from the alignments, were used to build a simplified graph by removing redundancies. Then, Gmove extracted all paths from the graph and searched for open reading frames consistent with the protein evidence. A selection step was applied to all candidate genes, essentially based on gene structure.</p><p>Finally, we used the pan-genomes of <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Golicz, A. A. et al. The pangenome of an agronomically important crop plant Brassica oleracea. Nat. Commun. 7, 13390 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d51054e3042">22</a></sup> and <i>B.</i> <i>napus</i> to complete the gene catalogue. We aligned these two protein databases using the same workflow and integrated the results using Gmove. The final gene catalogues of <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> and <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> are composed of the first Gmove results with the predicted genes (based on pan-genomes) that do not overlap with any previous annotation.</p><p>Using this pipeline, 46,721, 61,279 and 32,809 gene models were predicted for <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i>, <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> and <i>M.</i> <i>schizocarpa</i>, respectively. We assessed the completeness of the annotation using BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Waterhouse, R. M. et al. BUSCO applications from quality assessments to gene prediction and phylogenomics. Mol. Biol. Evol. 35, 543–548 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d51054e3087">52</a></sup> (embryophyta data set) and detected a similar (or higher) proportion of complete genes when compared with existing gene annotations (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#Tab1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">13</a>). Moreover, we computed the annotation evaluation distance for the three genomes using the existing gene catalogues as reference annotation (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">14</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Comparison with available plant assemblies</h3><p>We downloaded a selection of 102 plant genome assemblies by retrieving assemblies organized at the chromosome level and recently published genomes. We computed the usual metrics (cumulative size, NX, LX, average size, number and size of gaps) at the scaffold level. Where NX is defined as the sequence length of the shortest sequence at X% of the total genome length, and LX is defined as the smallest number of contigs whose length sum produces NX. Contigs were generated by fragmenting scaffolds at each N and metrics were computed from the resulting contig fasta files. Expected genome size and chromosome number information was obtained from the Kew website (<a href="https://www.kew.org">https://www.kew.org</a>) or from scientific publications. This information is available in Supplementary File <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM3">2</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec23">Comparison of ONT and PACBIO data sets</h3><p>Six PACBIO data sets were downloaded from the European Bioinformatics Institute-European Nucleotide Archive and metrics were computed from the whole data set with the following coverage: 127× (<i>V.</i> <i>angularis</i>, PRJDB3778), 231× (<i>V.</i> <i>vinifera</i>, PRJNA316730), 224× (<i>R.</i> <i>chinensis</i>, PRJNA413292), 125× (<i>C.</i> <i>maxima</i>, PRJNA318855), 168× (<i>F.</i> <i>vesca</i>, PRJNA383733) and 283× (<i>A.</i> <i>thaliana</i>, PRJNA237120). Likewise, metrics were computed from the whole ONT data sets: 79× (<i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i>), 51× (<i>M.</i> <i>schizocarpa</i>) and 36× (<i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i>). All of the standard metrics are available in Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">17</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec24">Comparison with reference genomes</h3><p>We compared the three genome assemblies with corresponding reference genomes using nucmer from the mummer4 package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Marcais, G. et al. MUMmer4: a fast and versatile genome alignment system. PLoS Comput. Biol. 14, e1005944 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d51054e3194">53</a></sup> and dot<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Nettstad M. Dot (DNA Nexus, 2017); &#xA;                    http://github.com/dnanexus/dot&#xA;                    &#xA;                  &#xA;                        " href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d51054e3198">54</a></sup>, an interactive dot plot viewer, to generate genome–genome alignment dot plots (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">4</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">6</a>).</p><p>We downloaded the 199 <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> and 119 <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> accessions from the NCBI website (PRJNA312457; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">1</a>), raw reads were then mapped using bwa mem (default parameters) to the chromosomes of the reference and our nanopore assemblies for the two species. The proportion of mapped reads for each accession was computed from the BAM output files (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">18</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">19</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">13</a>). We retrieved the 65.7 million and 188 million Illumina reads that could not be mapped to the reference genomes (To1000 or Chiifu). We were able to localize 52% and 39% of these reads to our assemblies, respectively. By screening the coverage along the HDEM and Z1 chromosomes, we found 2,735 and 3,508 regions (larger than 1 kb and with a coverage of at least 10×), respectively, representing 5.26 Mb and 7.61 Mb and spread over all of the chromosomes. We localized and annotated these regions as coding exon, intron or transposable element using bedtools (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">14</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">15</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">20</a>), and observed that nearly 20% of the bases (representing 1.14 Mb and 1.54 Mb for HDEM and Z1, respectively) were annotated as genic regions (intron + exon).</p><p>We computed best reciprocal hits using blastp between reference annotations and our gene predictions. We compared the gene order (synteny) for each couple of genome and searched for syntenic clusters of at least ten genes that are not located on the same chromosome. We found 1, 23 and 5 translocations between the <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i>, <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> and <i>Musa</i> (assembly V1) genomes involving 25, 951 and 658 genes, respectively. Synteny visualizations were performed using the MCScan tool, obtained from <a href="https://github.com/tanghaibao/jcvi/wiki/MCscan-(Python-version)">https://github.com/tanghaibao/jcvi/wiki/MCscan-(Python-version)</a> (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">7</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">9</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Estimation of sequencing costs</h3><p>We computed the sequencing costs for each genome using the following public prices: US$500 and US$2,200 for each MinION and PromethION flow cell, US$2,146 for one-third of a HiSeq2500 flow cell (2 × 250 bp), US$175 for nanopore long-read library preparation, US$51 for Illumina PCR-Free library preparation, US$1,500 for a single BioNano Genomics chip and US$474 for the library preparation for two optical maps (BspQI and DLE-1). Given these costs, we estimated sequencing costs of US$13,621, US$12,271 and US$16,321 to generate the chromosome-scale assemblies of <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i>, <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> and <i>M.</i> <i>schizocarpa</i>, respectively, based on the MinION device. The sequencing cost for the <i>M.</i> <i>schizocarpa</i> assembly based on the PromethION device was estimated to be US$6,546.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec26">Analysis of <i>FLC</i> genes</h3><p>The <i>FLC</i> genes were searched using blastp and a database of <i>FLC</i> genes composed of predicted genes from a previous study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Schranz, M. E. et al. Characterization and effects of the replicated flowering time gene FLC in Brassica rapa. Genetics 162, 1457–1468 (2002)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d51054e3329">23</a></sup> and the pan-genome of <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Golicz, A. A. et al. The pangenome of an agronomically important crop plant Brassica oleracea. Nat. Commun. 7, 13390 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d51054e3338">22</a></sup>. Five and three <i>FLC</i> genes were found in the gene catalogue of HDEM and Z1, respectively. The sixth and fourth genes were missing in the HDEM and Z1 annotation and were retrieved from the genomic sequence and added to the gene catalogue. Finally, we reported three <i>FLC1</i>, one disrupted <i>FLC2</i>, one <i>FLC3</i> and one <i>FLC5</i> genes for <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> HDEM and one <i>FLC1</i>, one <i>FLC2</i>, one <i>FLC3</i> and one <i>FLC5</i> genes for <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> Z1. Phylogenic trees were built using Phylogeny.fr<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Dereeper, A. et al. Phylogeny.fr: robust phylogenetic analysis for the non-specialist. Nucleic Acids Res. 36, W465–W469 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d51054e3384">55</a></sup>, a free, simple-to-use web service dedicated to reconstructing and analysing phylogenetic relationships between molecular sequences (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">17</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec27">Identification and analysis of the self-incompatibility locus in the two <i>Brassica</i> genomes</h3><p>Self-incompatibility emerged as an evolutionary strategy to foster genetic diversity and exchange in plant species. In Brassicaceae, self-incompatibility is controlled by a single multi-allelic locus (named the <i>S</i>-locus). We first identified the self-incompatibility alleles within each of the <i>Brassica</i> genomes with an unpublished <i>S</i>-locus genotyping pipeline using raw Illumina reads from shotgun sequencing of each individual (Z1 and HDEM) and a database of all available sequences of <i>SRK</i> (the self-incompatibility gene expressed in the pistil) from GenBank. Briefly, this pipeline (named NGSgenotyp) uses Bowtie2 to align raw reads against each reference sequence from the database and produces summary statistics with SAMtools (v1.4). We found that Z1 shared the same class II <i>S</i>-haplotype (<i>B.</i> <i>rapa S-60</i>) as Chiifu, whereas HDEM had a different class I <i>S</i>-haplotype (<i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> <i>S-13</i>) than To1000 (<i>B.</i> <i>oleracea S-28</i>). Using the full <i>SRK</i> sequences of these two <i>S</i>-alleles from GenBank, we localized the <i>S</i>-locus within each of the two <i>Brassica</i> assemblies with a blast search. We also identified the two other <i>S</i>-locus genes, <i>SCR/SP11</i>, the pollen-expressed gene, and <i>SLG</i>, a pistil-expressed <i>SRK</i> paralogue, within each assembly. Note that HDEM has full coding sequences of <i>SRK</i> and <i>SCR</i>, whereas To1000 apparently lacks the <i>SCR</i> gene as well as the first two exons of <i>SRK</i>. Then, we analysed the synteny in the <i>S</i>-locus and in flanking regions by comparative analyses (using blast) of the annotated assemblies with the corresponding <i>S</i>-locus regions in the <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> and <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> reference genomes. Analysis of the two pairs of <i>S</i>-locus sequences revealed high sequence homology in genic and intergenic regions for the Z1–Chiifu pair as they share the same <i>S</i>-allele, whereas low overall homology was found for the HDEM–To1000 pair, which has distinct <i>S</i>-alleles. Finally, we produced a figure representing the annotated genes within the <i>S</i>-locus and its flanking regions using R and used mVista to estimate the degree of sequence homology in the <i>S</i>-locus region between our two <i>Brassica</i> assemblies and the two reference genomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">19</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec28">Analysis of assembly completeness of <i>R</i>-gene clusters</h3><p>Three orthologous disease resistance-like genes clusters identified in DH-Pahang assembly version 1 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="D’Hont, A. et al. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. Nature 488, 213–217 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d51054e3535">16</a></sup>) were searched in DH-Pahang version 2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Martin, G. et al. Improvement of the banana “Musa acuminata” reference sequence using NGS data and semi-automated bioinformatics methods. BMC Genomics 17, 243 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d51054e3539">17</a></sup>) and in the assembly of <i>M.</i> <i>schizocarpa</i> with gene identifiers and blast search, respectively. Cluster boundaries were manually refined based on the gene annotation of these two genomes and the proportion of N was calculated for each region (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM1">21</a>). The proportions of N reported in results were calculated as the N sum in the three clusters divided by their cumulated size.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec29">Reporting Summary</h3><p>Further information on experimental design is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4#MOESM2">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>The genome assemblies, gene predictions and genome browsers are freely available at <a href="http://www.genoscope.cns.fr/plants">http://www.genoscope.cns.fr/plants</a>. The Illumina, MinION and PromethION data, the assemblies and the annotations are available in the European Nucleotide Archive under the following projects: <a href="http://www.ebi.ac.uk/ena/data/view/PRJEB26620">PRJEB26620</a> (<i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i>), <a href="http://www.ebi.ac.uk/ena/data/view/PRJEB26621">PRJEB26621</a> (<i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i>) and <a href="http://www.ebi.ac.uk/ena/data/view/PRJEB26661">PRJEB26661</a> (<i>M.</i> <i>schizocarpa</i>). Germplasm for these genomes will be made freely and publicly available to the entire community. <i>M.</i> <i>schizocarpa</i> germplasm is available at Bioversity International Transit Center under ITC number ITC0926. <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> ssp. <i>trilocularis</i> (genotype Z1) is available at the Plant Genetic Resources of Canada and <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> ssp. <i>italica</i> (genotype HDEM) is available at the Biological Resource Center BrACySol, Rennes, France. All supporting data are included in the Supplementary Information.</p>

C.C. and A.L. optimized and performed the sequencing. E.D., W.B. and V.B. generated the optical maps. P.D., R.D. and M.M.-D. generated the genetic map for the <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> HDEM accession. B.I., C.B. and J.-M.A. performed the genome assemblies. G.M. performed the anchoring of the <i>M.</i> <i>schizocarpa</i> scaffolds. C.F., J.M. and M.R.-G. performed the anchoring of the <i>B.</i> <i>oleracea</i> scaffolds. M.D. and J.-M.A. performed the anchoring of the <i>B.</i> <i>rapa</i> scaffolds. M.D. and B.N. performed the gene prediction for the genome assemblies. B.I., C.B., M.D., F.D., J.-M.A. and S.E. performed the bioinformatic analyses. X.V. and M.G. performed the <i>S</i>-locus annotation of the two Brassicaceae genomes. B.I., C.B., M.D. and J.-M.A. wrote the article. A.D., A.-M.C., P.W. and J.-M.A. supervised the study.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding author</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to
                <a id="corresp-c1" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41477-018-0289-4/email/correspondent/c1/new">Jean-Marc Aury</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="ethics" data-title="Ethics declarations"><div class="c-article-section" id="ethics-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="ethics">Ethics declarations</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="ethics-content">
              
                <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="FPar1">Competing interests</h3>
                <p>The authors declare no competing interests. B.I., S.E., C.C., P.W. and J.-M.A. are part of the MinION Access Programme and J.-M.A. received travel and accommodation expenses to speak at ONT conferences.</p>
